        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259"><meta property="og:title" content="Low-Cost and High-Performance Hard Carbon Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/asset/images/acsomega.7b00259.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="As an anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), hard carbon (HC) presents high specific capacity and favorable cycling performance. However, high cost and low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of HC seriously limit its future commercialization for SIBs. A typical biowaste, mangosteen shell was selected as a precursor to prepare low-cost and high-performance HC via a facile one-step carbonization method, and the influence of different heat treatments on the morphologies, microstructures, and electrochemical performances was investigated systematically. The microstructure evolution studied using X-ray diffraction, Raman, Brunauer–Emmett–Teller, and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy, along with electrochemical measurements, reveals the optimal carbonization condition of the mangosteen shell: HC carbonized at 1500 °C for 2 h delivers the highest reversible capacity of ∼330 mA h g–1 at a current density of 20 mA g–1, a capacity retention of ∼98% after 100 cycles, and an ICE of ∼83%. Additionally, the sodium-ion storage behavior of HC is deeply analyzed using galvanostatic intermittent titration and cyclic voltammetry technologies."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/asset/images/acsomega.7b00259.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90f6ef801bf7775c-LHR"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6ef801bf7775c-LHR">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'baa8567f-da42-4a25-89ff-7c3cd5344e24'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6ef801bf7775c-LHR">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'omega';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/omega/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/omega/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

$(function() {
    var serialCode = "acsodf";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Low-Cost and High-Performance Hard Carbon Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.7b00259", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Low-Cost and High-Performance Hard Carbon Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.7b00259", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Low-Cost and High-Performance Hard Carbon Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.7b00259", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Low-Cost and High-Performance Hard Carbon Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.7b00259", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Low-Cost and High-Performance Hard Carbon Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.7b00259", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Low-Cost and High-Performance Hard Carbon Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.7b00259", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Low-Cost and High-Performance Hard Carbon Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.7b00259", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Low-Cost and High-Performance Hard Carbon Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.7b00259", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Low-Cost and High-Performance Hard Carbon Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.7b00259", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

This publication is licensed under these
                            <a href="https://pubs.acs.org/page/policy/authorchoice_termsofuse.html" class="link--bold">Terms of Use</a>.
                        </p></div></div><div class="article_rightPermissionsLink"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/page/rightslinkno.jsp" data-id="article_rights_permissions">Request reuse permissions</a></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/asset/images/medium/ao-2017-00259x_0006.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/asset/images/large/ao-2017-00259x_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.7b00259&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">As an anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), hard carbon (HC) presents high specific capacity and favorable cycling performance. However, high cost and low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of HC seriously limit its future commercialization for SIBs. A typical biowaste, mangosteen shell was selected as a precursor to prepare low-cost and high-performance HC via a facile one-step carbonization method, and the influence of different heat treatments on the morphologies, microstructures, and electrochemical performances was investigated systematically. The microstructure evolution studied using X-ray diffraction, Raman, Brunauer–Emmett–Teller, and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy, along with electrochemical measurements, reveals the optimal carbonization condition of the mangosteen shell: HC carbonized at 1500 °C for 2 h delivers the highest reversible capacity of ∼330 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at a current density of 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, a capacity retention of ∼98% after 100 cycles, and an ICE of ∼83%. Additionally, the sodium-ion storage behavior of HC is deeply analyzed using galvanostatic intermittent titration and cyclic voltammetry technologies.</p></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2017 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291442&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Anode materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Anode%20materials" class="article__tags__link">Anode materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Batteries" class="article__tags__link">Batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291338&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrical properties" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrical%20properties" class="article__tags__link">Electrical properties</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292439&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Physical and chemical processes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Physical%20and%20chemical%20processes" class="article__tags__link">Physical and chemical processes</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Currently, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted considerable attention for large-scale energy storage because of the abundance of sodium resources throughout the world. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1-3)</a> However, the development of low-cost and high-performance electrode materials is the key to commercializing sodium-based batteries. Fortunately, a series of high-performance cathode materials for SIBs have been discovered, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 4 and 5">(4, 5)</a> including layered oxides (P2- and O3-type layered oxides), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 6 to 11">(6-11)</a> tunnel-type oxides, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> phosphates, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 13 to 16">(13-16)</a> and sulfates. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> In particular, Prussian blue analogues with a high reversible capacity (RC) of ∼160 mA g<sup>–1</sup> are ideal for commercial applications because of their simple preparation and low cost. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 18 to 22">(18-22)</a> Recently, high-capacity P2-type Na<sub>0.67</sub>[Fe<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> and high-voltage polyanionic Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> have been studied as novel cathode materials for SIBs, which show specific capacities comparable with the current commercial cathodes in lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) currently used for commercial small-scale applications, including hand-held devices like phones and tablets.</div><div class="NLM_p">In addition, anode materials for SIBs have also been widely studied, including alloys, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 24 to 28">(24-28)</a> oxides, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29 ref30 ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 29 to 31">(29-31)</a> organic compounds, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32 ref33 ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 32 to 34">(32-34)</a> and carbon materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44 ref45 ref46 ref47 ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44 ref45 ref46 ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 35 to 48">(35-48)</a> The big volume expansion of alloy anodes during sodium insertion results in the loss of electric contact and fast capacity fading. The capacities of oxide anodes are very small. Organic compounds, for example disodium terephthalate (Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>4</sub>O<sub>4</sub>), suffer from poor cycling stability, low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE), and insufficient electronic conductivity.</div><div class="NLM_p">As we know, graphite is the most widely used anode material in LIBs because of its abundant resource, excellent electronic conductivity, low average potential, and outstanding cycling stability. Unfortunately, graphite shows poor electrochemical performance as an anode material for SIBs using traditional carbonate electrolytes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39 ref44 ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref44 ref49" aria-label="reference 39, 44 and 49">(39, 44, 49)</a> Recently, carbonate electrolytes have been substituted by ether-based electrolytes to obtain a RC of ∼150 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> in SIBs using a graphite anode, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50 ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref51" aria-label="reference 50 and 51">(50, 51)</a> but the capacity is still far from satisfying the practical needs.</div><div class="NLM_p">To date, hard carbon (HC) is one of the most promising anode materials for SIBs because of its low average potential and high RC. Thus, HCs prepared from glucose, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> PAN fibers, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52 ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52 ref53" aria-label="reference 52 and 53">(52, 53)</a> phenolic resin (PF), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> resorcinol formaldehyde (RF) resin, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55 ref56 ref57'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55 ref56 ref57" aria-label="reference 55 to 57">(55-57)</a> and other polymers <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48 ref58'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48 ref58" aria-label="reference 48 and 58">(48, 58)</a> have attracted much research interests; especially, PF is one kind of flexible precursor leading to high-purity HC and allowing the production of carbon materials with controllable microstructures and morphologies. As reported, HCs derived from PF has a perfect spherule shape, large interlayer distance, and disordered microstructure, which bring about sodium-ion intercalation and storage with a capacity of ∼310 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and long-cycle electrochemical stability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">For developing low-cost HC anode for SIBs, many biomass sources—such as silk, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> banana peels, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> peat moss, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> pomelo peels, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref59'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59" aria-label="reference 59">(59)</a> lignin, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref60 ref61'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref60 ref61" aria-label="reference 60 and 61">(60, 61)</a> sucrose, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> and cellulose nanofiber <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref62'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref62" aria-label="reference 62">(62)</a>—have been used as precursors, and their derived carbons demonstrate suitable performance as anodes for SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p">However, for now, there are still two keys to facilitate industrialized processing of biomass-based HCs: (1) a general carbonization technology leading to the best performance and high yields from various biomass sources with the lowest energy consumption and (2) a comprehensive understanding of sodium-ion storage behaviors in the different biomass-based HCs.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In most of the preceding studies, carbonization temperature has been extensively investigated, but carbonization time—a key factor for using less energy—has been always ignored. In this work, mangosteen shell, a typical biowaste, was carbonized through a systemic heat treatment process. The as-prepared HCs at different heating temperatures and times reveal discrepant sodium-ion storage behaviors. The sodium-ion storage behavior varies from chemi-/physisorption on surface functional groups and heteroatoms to the insertion process resulting in disordered carbon nanovoids, which display different potentials and capacities, respectively. A possible sodium-ion storage mechanism in HC is proposed.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p"><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/suppl_file/ao7b00259_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a> shows the digital photograph and scanning electron microscopy (SEM) image (after mechanical crushing) of the mangosteen shell precursor. The reddish-brown mangosteen shell turns into brown powder after being dried and smashed. The as-prepared powder turns black after pyrolysis, and the carbon yields under different carbonization conditions are listed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/suppl_file/ao7b00259_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>. SEM images of HCs carbonized at different temperature and time are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/suppl_file/ao7b00259_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S2 and S3</a>, respectively, and all of the HC particles maintain irregular shapes with smooth surfaces, which means that the different carbonization process has little effect on the particle size and surface morphology.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a,c shows X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of HC-Ts carbonized at different temperatures and HC-hs carbonized for different times. All XRD patterns exhibit two broad peaks at ∼24° and ∼43°, which are assigned to the crystallographic planes of (002) and (101) in the carbon structure, respectively. Obviously, with increasing carbonization temperature, the (002) peak of HC-Ts becomes sharper and shifts right significantly, which indicates not only an increase in long-range order degree but also a decrease in HC-Ts interplanar spacing (<i>d</i><sub>002</sub>). Differently, with increasing carbonization time from 0.5 to 5 h at 1500 °C, the (002) peak of HC-hs roughly maintains the shape. Only its position is altered, which shifts to a higher angle; this suggests that the change in interplanar spacing is more obvious than that on the order degree in HC-hs with prolonged sintering time.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/asset/images/medium/ao-2017-00259x_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/asset/images/large/ao-2017-00259x_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Structure of HC carbonized under different conditions. (a) XRD patterns and (b) Raman spectra normalized by the peak height of G-band of HC carbonized at temperatures ranging from 800–1600 °C for 2 h. (c) XRD patterns and (d) Raman spectra normalized by the peak height of G-band of HC carbonized at 1500 °C for 0.5–5 h.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/asset/images/large/ao-2017-00259x_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.7b00259&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">On the basis of the Bragg equation 2<i>d</i> sin θ = <i>n</i>λ, the <i>d</i><sub>002</sub> values of HC-Ts are shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#tbl1" aria-label="Table 1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>. With increasing carbonization temperature, the <i>d</i><sub>002</sub> value shrinks gradually from 3.84 to 3.66 Å, but this is still much larger than that of graphite (∼3.4 Å) and ensures facile sodium-ion insertion/extraction between the graphene layers. The average thicknesses of graphitic domains (<i>L</i><sub>c</sub>) were obtained using the Scherrer equation with the peak positions and full width at half-maximum (FWHM) values of (002) peak. <i>L</i><sub>c</sub> increases slightly with increasing carbonization temperature. According to the <i>d</i><sub>002</sub> value and <i>L</i><sub>c</sub>, the number of interlayers stocked in the graphitic domains can be roughly estimated. For instance, the <i>L</i><sub>c</sub> and <i>d</i><sub>002</sub> of HC1500C2h are 1.64 nm and 3.67 Å, respectively, indicating that the graphitic domain of HC1500C2h is made up of 5–6 stacked graphene layers (<i>n</i> = 1.64/0.367 + 1). The number of the stocked interlayers in graphitic domains increases slowly from 4–5 to 5–6 as the carbonization temperature increases from 800 to 1600 °C.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. Structure Parameters and Electrochemical Properties of HC Carbonized at Different Temperatures for 2 h</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap"><table class="table" class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">HC1300C2h</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.71</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">2.63</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1.58</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.598</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">81.5</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">280</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">74</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">HC1400C2h</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.69</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">2.75</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1.61</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.624</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">38.8</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">300</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">80</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">HC1500C2h</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.67</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">2.93</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1.64</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.666</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">8.9</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">330</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">83</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">HC1600C2h</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.66</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">2.94</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1.65</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.668</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">2.4</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">270</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">86</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class="NLM_table-wrap-foot" id="IDtable-wrap-foot-t1fn1"><div class="footnote" id="t1fn1"><sup>a</sup><p class="last"><i>I</i><sub>D</sub> and <i>I</i><sub>G</sub> are the integrated intensities of the D and G bands, respectively.</p></div><div class="footnote" id="t1fn2"><sup>b</sup><p class="last">Surface area was calculated using Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) method.</p></div><div class="footnote" id="t1fn3"><sup>c</sup><p class="last">The reversible capacity (RC).</p></div><div class="footnote" id="t1fn4"><sup>d</sup><p class="last">The initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE).</p></div></div></div><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b,d displays the Raman spectra of HCs normalized by the peak height of G-band. All of them show two bands located at ∼1350 and ∼1600 cm<sup>–1</sup> corresponding to the defect induced D band and the crystalline graphite G band of carbon, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref63 ref64'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref63 ref64" aria-label="reference 63 and 64">(63, 64)</a> respectively. The comparative intensity ratio of the G band and D band (<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>) is used to quantify the disorder degree of carbon materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36 ref63 ref64'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref63 ref64" aria-label="reference 36, 63 and 64">(36, 63, 64)</a><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/suppl_file/ao7b00259_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a> shows the fitting Raman curves of HC-Ts, and the calculated value of <i>I</i><sub>G</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>D</sub> is shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#tbl1" aria-label="Table 1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>. Interestingly, with increasing carbonization temperature, the intensity of D band increases, but its FWHM gradually decreases, and the value of <i>I</i><sub>G</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>D</sub> keeps increasing, which shows a change in the structure from amorphous to planar graphite microlites. In addition, <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d shows the Raman spectra of HC-hs. With increasing carbonization time, the peak intensity of D band decreases, and the value of <i>I</i><sub>G</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>D</sub> increases continuously (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/suppl_file/ao7b00259_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>), indicating the promoted graphitization degree. The results comport with the XRD results.</div><div class="NLM_p">The high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HR-TEM) images of HC-Ts are displayed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a–d [inset selected area electron diffraction (SAED)]. The disordered structure observed shows a degree of amorphous of HC-Ts. The results suggest that the local ordered structures, which represent nanographite domains, become more distinct at a higher carbonization temperature. All SAED patterns exhibit dispersive diffraction rings, which further confirm the disordered microstructure of HC-Ts. The diffraction ring becomes sharper with increasing carbonization temperature, which demonstrates a tendency toward ordered structure. In addition, some graphitic domains arise in the local ordered structure of HC800C2h. With increasing carbonization temperature, the graphitic domains become more apparent in HC1300C2h combined with an appearance of nanovoids surrounded by some parallel carbon hexagonal layers. The maximum amount of nanovoids with a size of ∼1 nm is achieved in HC1500C2h (see <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c) and a minimum amount at 1100 °C, which is due to the random packing and agglomerations of carbon layers at a higher temperature. Subsequently, both increasing temperature and extending time, the amount of such nanovoids will drop, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d–f.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/asset/images/medium/ao-2017-00259x_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/asset/images/large/ao-2017-00259x_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. HR-TEM images of (a) HC800C2h, (b) HC1300C2h, (c) HC1500C2h, (d) HC1600C2h, (e) HC1500C0.5h, and (f) HC1500C5h.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/asset/images/large/ao-2017-00259x_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.7b00259&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/suppl_file/ao7b00259_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6a–e</a> displays the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in a voltage range of 0–2 V. A small reduction peak located at ∼0.5 V is observed in the first scan and disappears in the subsequent cycles, which is attributed to an irreversible reaction of the electrolyte with surface functional groups of the active material and formation of the solid–electrolyte interface (SEI) layer. As we know, the formation of the SEI layer and other irreversible reactions directly result in a low ICE. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#tbl1" aria-label="Table 1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a> shows that the ICE of HC-Ts improves remarkably with increasing carbonization temperature. The results show that the larger specific surface area (see <a class="ref internalNav" href="#tbl1" aria-label="Table 1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/suppl_file/ao7b00259_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>) and higher amount of functional groups remained at a lower carbonization temperature, thus leading to a lower ICE.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a shows the typical initial galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of HC-Ts at a current density of 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup> in a voltage range of 0–2 V. The specific capacities of HC-Ts are ∼70, 220, 280, 330, and 270 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. All charge/discharge profiles except for HC800C2h show a flat plateau region at a potential ∼0.2 V and a sloping region in the range of 0.2–1 V (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/suppl_file/ao7b00259_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>). The effect of carbonization temperature on the plateau region capacity (PRC) and sloping region capacity (SRC) is summed up in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b. With increasing carbonization temperature, the SRC decreases slowly and the PRC significantly increases with the highest value obtained at 1500 °C. On the basis of the results of XRD, Raman, and HR-TEM, HC-Ts have less defect, more nanographite, and nanovoids at higher carbonization temperature. Therefore, the results suggest that the SRC comes from sodium-ion storage in the defects, edges, and surface of HC-Ts, whereas the PRC is produced by sodium-ion storage in the interlayer of nanographite and nanovoids formed by nanographite. At a lower carbonization temperature, HC-Ts contain larger specific surface area, as well as a higher number of surface functional groups, heteroatoms, and defects. Thus, an absorption/desorption behavior of sodium-ion storage is displayed in HC800C2h.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/asset/images/medium/ao-2017-00259x_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/asset/images/large/ao-2017-00259x_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Electrochemical performance of HC carbonized at different temperatures for 2 h. (a) Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles obtained at 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup> in the voltage range of 0–2 V. (b) Summarized PRC and SRC in the first charge at 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Cycling performance at 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Charge capacity at various current densities.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/asset/images/large/ao-2017-00259x_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.7b00259&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">However, there are few nanographite domains and nanovoids, both of which contribute greatly to sodium storage capacity. Undoubtedly, HC800C2h shows a much lower capacity compared with HC pyrolyzed at higher temperatures.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c shows the cycling performance of HC-Ts at a constant current density of 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. All of the HC-Ts anodes deliver excellent cycling stability. The highest specific capacity of ∼330 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> is obtained in HC1500C2h with a capacity retention of ∼98% after 100 cycles. The superior cycling performance of HC-Ts is attributed to their unique stable 3D disordered structures formed at high carbonization temperatures. A comparison of the electrochemical performances between our mangosteen shell based HCs and some reported biomass-based HCs is listed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/suppl_file/ao7b00259_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>, in which our HC delivers preferable ICE and RC.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d shows the rate capability of HC-Ts anodes tested at various current densities from 20 to 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. All HC-Ts anodes deliver good rate performance, and HC1500C2h delivers the highest specific capacity at low current densities, whereas HC1300C2h exhibits the best rate capability. Importantly, all HC-Ts anodes retain their high reversible capacities when the current density decreases from 200 to 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, which indicates that the high-rate cycling does not perpetually destroy the microstructures of HC-Ts and their capacity can restore to the original structure at a small current density.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a displays the charge/discharge profiles of HC-hs at a current density of 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup> in the voltage range of 0–2 V. HC1500C2h delivers the highest RC of ∼330 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with the highest PRC ≈ 250 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and ICE ≈ 83% in the electrochemical curves (see <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/suppl_file/ao7b00259_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>, respectively). This is due to the most number of sodium-ion storage sites in the HC1500C2h anode material, which is well-supported by all characterizations in particular from HR-TEM. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c, all HC-hs anodes deliver excellent cycling performance with capacity retention over 92% after 100 cycles at the current density of 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup> and a high ICE ≈ 80%.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/asset/images/medium/ao-2017-00259x_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/asset/images/large/ao-2017-00259x_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Electrochemical performance of HC carbonized at 1500 °C for different times. (a) Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles obtained at 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup> in the voltage range of 0–2 V. (b) Summarized PRC and SRC in the first charge at 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Cycling performance at 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Charge capacity at various current densities.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/asset/images/large/ao-2017-00259x_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.7b00259&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d shows the rate performance of the HC-hs at various current densities from 20 to 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Interestingly, HC1500C2h shows not only the highest specific capacity at a low-current density of 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup> but also the best rate performance among the HC-hs anodes. This point is very important for the future commercialization of anodes for SIBs. Specifically, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/suppl_file/ao7b00259_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a> shows the change in rules on the influence of carbonization temperature and time on the PRC and SRC, respectively, under various current densities. Interestingly, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/suppl_file/ao7b00259_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10a,c</a> shows a linear relationship between PRC and the current density when the current is less than 120 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. However, the impact of the current density on SRC is less than PRC (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/suppl_file/ao7b00259_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10b</a>), which is attributed to the different sodium-ion storage mechanisms in these two voltage regions and the severe polarization at high current rates.</div><div class="NLM_p">For insight into sodium-ion storage mechanisms of HCs, galvanostatic intermittent titration (GITT) was used to measure sodium-ion diffusion coefficient. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a shows the GITT curves of HC1500C2h anode at the second cycle from 0 to 2.0 V. During the GITT tests, the sodiation and desodiation were carried out at a constant current density of 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup> for 10 min, followed by an open-circuit stand for value (<i>E</i><sub>s</sub>). The sodium-ion diffusion coefficient of HC1500C2h is determined by solving Fick’s second law of diffusion using the following equation through a series of assumptions and simplifications <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref65 ref66 ref67 ref68'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref65 ref66 ref67 ref68" aria-label="reference 65 to 68">(65-68)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq1"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/asset/images/ao-2017-00259x_m001.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></span>where <i>M</i><sub>B</sub> and <i>m</i><sub>B</sub> are the molecular weight and mass of the electrode material, <i>L</i> is the thickness of the electrode, <i>S</i> is the surface area of the active material estimated from Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) data, <i>V</i><sub>B</sub> is the molar volume of the electrode material, and τ is the current duration time. When there is a good linear relationship between cell voltage and τ<sup>1/2</sup> during titration (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/suppl_file/ao7b00259_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>), <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a> can be simplified as<span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq2"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/asset/images/ao-2017-00259x_m002.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></span></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/asset/images/medium/ao-2017-00259x_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/asset/images/large/ao-2017-00259x_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. GITT test for HC1500C2h. (a) GITT charge/discharge profiles at the second cycle. (b) Potential profile before, during, and after a constant current pulse with schematic labeling of different parameters. Na<sup>+</sup> ion ADCs estimated from the GITT charge/discharge profiles for (c) sodiation and (d) desodiation processes at the second cycle.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.7b00259/asset/images/large/ao-2017-00259x_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.7b00259&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To simplify the calculation, <i>V</i><sub>M</sub> is assumed to be a constant throughout the electrochemical process. Δ<i>E</i><sub>s</sub> and Δ<i>E</i><sub>τ</sub> are obtained from the GITT curves following the method provided by the current step diagram shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c,d, the sodium-ion apparent diffusion coefficient (ADC) of HC1500C2h is on the order of 10<sup>–9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> during the sodiation and desodiation processes. The inset of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c is the data in a low-voltage region of ∼0.1 V. During the sodiation process, sodium-ion ADC undergoes a slow decrease to the demarcation point at ∼0.1 V followed by a sharp decrease. When the cell voltage reaches ∼0.06 V, the electrode delivers the minimum sodium-ion ADC on the order of 10<sup>–13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> until the voltage of ∼0.04 V, at which point sodium-ion ADC increases largely and then decreases until the cutoff voltage. For the desodiation process, sodium-ion ADC first decreases dramatically and then increases sharply to ∼0.1 V, followed by a relatively slow decrease until the cutoff voltage (see <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d). The low- and high-voltage sides of the first inflection point appearing at ∼0.1 V in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c,d correspond to the plateau region and sloping region on the charge/discharge profiles, respectively, indicating that the emergence of an inflection suggests a new starting electrochemical process.</div><div class="NLM_p">It could be concluded that the lowest sodium-ion ADC mainly concentrates at the plateau region of charge/discharge profiles. The extremely low diffusion coefficient in the plateau region indicates that the electrochemical reactions in this section are severely sluggish. This phenomenon could be ascribed to the high kinetic barriers related to the concurrent insertion/extraction of sodium-ions and structural development, which offers a reasonable interpretation of the severe capacity fading in the plateau region at high current densities. During the sodiation process, there is a sudden increase in sodium-ion ADC at ∼0.04 V, which suggests a new electrochemical behavior, sodium metal-nanovoids filling, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref67 ref69'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref67 ref69" aria-label="reference 67 and 69">(67, 69)</a> arising at this point. Similarly, sodium metal-nanovoids filling is located at ∼0.01 V and holds lower kinetic barriers relative to insertion/extraction. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53 ref70 ref71'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53 ref70 ref71" aria-label="reference 53, 70 and 71">(53, 70, 71)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">To summarize, physical and microstructure characterizations, electrochemical tests, and GITT results of HC reveal that the SRC comes from sodium-ion storage in the defects, edges, and surface, whereas the PRC is produced by sodium-ion storage in interlayer of nanographite and nanovoids formed by nanographite.</div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">HC materials have been successfully synthesized from biowaste mangosteen shell via a simple, one-step carbonization process. The precursors were carbonized at different temperatures from 800 to 1600 °C for 2 h and at different times from 0.5 to 5 h at 1500 °C. The as-prepared HCs exhibit different microstructure characteristics and electrochemical performances. Structural evolution studied using XRD, Raman, BET, and HR-TEM, along with electrochemical measurements, together obtain an optimal carbonization condition of 1500 °C for 2 h. HC1500C2h delivers the highest RC of ∼330 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (consisting of SRC ≈ 80 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and PRC ≈ 250 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) at a current density of 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the capacity retention of ∼98% after 100 cycles, and the ICE of ∼83%. In addition, the different sodium-ion storage mechanisms related to SRC and PRC were deeply analyzed using GITT and CV technologies to understand the sodium-ion storage behaviors in the biomass-based HC anodes. This work is beneficial to discover an optimal carbonization technology for various biomass precursors as anodes for SIBs and to deepen the understanding of a universal sodium-ion storage behavior in carbon-based materials.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i5">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4-1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Materials Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The collected mangosteen shell was cut into small pieces, dried, and grounded with a micromill. The obtained powder was loaded into a tubular furnace and carbonized at a given temperature for a certain time at a heating rate of 3 °C min<sup>–1</sup> under an argon flow. More specifically, the powder was pyrolyzed at a temperature range of 800–1600 °C (interval of 100 °C) for 2 h (noted as HC-Ts) and at 1500 °C for times of 0.5, 1, 2, 3, 5 h (noted as HC-hs). The as-synthesized HC-Ts and HC-hs are uniformly named by HC<i>x</i>C<i>y</i>h, where <i>x</i> represents sintering temperature and <i>y</i> represents sintering time.</div></div><div id="sec4-2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Materials Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The morphology of the as-synthesized HCs was observed using a VGA-3-SBH scanning electron microscope. The specific surface area and pore structure of the HCs were characterized using a Micromeritics ASAP 2020 analyzer. HR-TEM and SAED observations were performed using a JEOL JEM-2100F TEM instrument. XRD patterns were recorded using a PANalytical X’Pert instrument equipped with Cu Kα radiation. Raman spectra were recorded using a Renishaw Invia spectrometer with an Ar laser of 514.5 nm.</div></div><div id="sec4-3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">A slurry containing 80% active material, 10% carbon black (Super-P), and 10% polyacrylic acid sodium (binder) was dispersed in H<sub>2</sub>O. The obtained slurry was coated onto a Cu foil and then dried at 120 °C overnight in a vacuum oven. The active material mass loading of the electrodes was ∼2.50 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. 2032-type coin cells were assembled with the working electrode, sodium counter electrode, glass fiber separator, and sufficient electrolyte in an argon-filled glovebox. The electrolyte was 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethylene carbonate (EC) and propylene carbonate (PC) (1:1 by volume). The charge/discharge measurements were taken using a Land BT2000 system (Wuhan, China). CV was carried out using a PMC-500 instrument at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> within a voltage range of 0–2 V. GITT measurements were also taken using a Land BT2000 system under a 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup> current pulse with 10 min duration and 40 min rest interval.</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">Supporting                         cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Towards greener and more sustainable batteries for electrical energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Larcher, D.; Tarascon, J.-M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Chemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">19-29</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Ever-growing energy needs and depleting fossil-fuel resources demand the pursuit of sustainable energy alternatives, including both renewable energy sources and sustainable storage technologies.  It is therefore essential to incorporate material abundance, eco-efficient synthetic processes and life-cycle anal. into the design of new electrochem. storage systems.  At present, a few existing technologies address these issues, but in each case, fundamental and technol. hurdles remain to be overcome.  Here the authors provide an overview of the current state of energy storage from a sustainability perspective.  The authors introduce the notion of sustainability through discussion of the energy and environmental costs of state-of-the-art Li-ion batteries, considering elemental abundance, toxicity, synthetic methods and scalability.  With the same themes in mind, the authors also highlight current and future electrochem. storage systems beyond Li-ion batteries.  The complexity and importance of recycling battery materials is also discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGph4-d2B3Q8DLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgxW7tivbPJYw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhvFKlsrjF&amp;md5=977f0fc171bb41af30457715fc0ded72</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1039/C2EE02781J"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Palomares, V.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Serras, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Villaluenga, I.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hueso, K.                         cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-ion batteries, recent advances and present challenges to become low cost energy storage systems</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Palomares, Veronica; Serras, Paula; Villaluenga, Irune; Hueso, Karina B.; Carretero-Gonzalez, Javier; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoRYtxfVVW_sLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgxW7tivbPJYw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XivFWnu7s%253D&amp;md5=72869139086b8ee9adec746418ff84b1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1021/cr500192f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yabuuchi, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kubota, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dahbi, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Komaba, S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Research Development on Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem.                 10.1021/cr500192f</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D114%26publication_year%3D2014%26pages%3D11636-11682%26journal%3DChem.%2BRev.%26author%3DN.%2BYabuuchi%26author%3DK.%2BKubota%26author%3DM.%2BDahbi%26author%3DS.%2BKomaba%26title%3DResearch%2BDevelopment%2Bon%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1021%252Fcr500192f&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsomega.7b00259&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Fcr500192f&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Research Development on Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yabuuchi, Naoaki; Kubota, Kei; Dahbi, Mouad; Komaba, Shinichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Reviews (Washington, DC, United States)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">114</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">23</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">11636-11682</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CHREAY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0009-2665</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review of sodium-ion battery technol.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent Advances and Prospects of Cathode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xiang, Xingde; Zhang, Kai; Chen, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">27</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">36</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5343-5364</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADVMEW</span>;
