                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref47"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">47</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit47" data-doi="10.1021/acs.jpcc.5b05443"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Qiao, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Du, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhou, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dai, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bao, J.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Fluorine-Doped Carbon Particles Derived from Lotus Petioles as High-Performance Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J. Phys. Chem. C</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">119</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">21336</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">21344</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acs.jpcc.5b05443</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D119%26publication_year%3D2015%26pages%3D21336-21344%26journal%3DJ.%2BPhys.%2BChem.%2BC%26author%3DP.%2BWang%26author%3DB.%2BQiao%26author%3DY.%2BDu%26author%3DY.%2BLi%26author%3DX.%2BZhou%26author%3DZ.%2BDai%26author%3DJ.%2BBao%26title%3DFluorine-Doped%2BCarbon%2BParticles%2BDerived%2Bfrom%2BLotus%2BPetioles%2Bas%2BHigh-Performance%2BAnode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facs.jpcc.5b05443&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsomega.7b00259&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facs.jpcc.5b05443&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit47R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">47</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Fluorine-Doped Carbon Particles Derived from Lotus Petioles as High-Performance Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Pengzi; Qiao, Bin; Du, Yichen; Li, Yafei; Zhou, Xiaosi; Dai, Zhihui; Bao, Jianchun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">119</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">37</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">21336-21344</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPCCCK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1932-7447</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In contrast to the extensive study of the electrochem. performance of conventional C materials in Na-ion batteries, there was scarcely any study of Na storage property of F-doped C.  Here the authors report for the 1st time the application of F-doped C particles (F-CP) synthesized through pyrolysis of lotus petioles as anode materials for Na-ion batteries.  Electrochem. tests demonstrate that the F-CP electrode delivers an initial charge capacity of 230 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 50 mA g-1 between 0.001 and 2.8 V, which greatly outperforms the corresponding value of 149 mA h g-1 for the counterpart banana peels-derived C (BPC).  Even under 200 mA g-1, the F-CP electrode could still exhibit a charge capacity of 228 mA h g-1 with initial charge capacity retention of 99.1% after 200 cycles compared to the BPC electrode with 107 mA h g-1 and 71.8%.  The F-doping and the large interlayer distance as well as the disorder structure contribute to a lowering of the Na ion insertion-extn. barrier, thus promoting the Na+ diffusion and providing more active sites for Na+ storage.  In specific, the F-CP electrode shows longer low-discharge-plateau and better kinetics than does the common C-based electrode.  The unique electrochem. performance of F-CP enriches the existing knowledge of the C-based electrode materials and broadens avenues for rational design of anode materials in Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoQqQAaLqKEFLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhVN4Vew7GenA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhsVeqsb7I&amp;md5=8b9052e5c058808551c562c94d9a5a64</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref48"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">48</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit48" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2012.12.072"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Qie, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yuan, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Huang, Y.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Functionalized N-doped Interconnected Carbon Nanofibers as an Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Storage with Excellent Performance</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Carbon</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">55</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">328</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">334</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. performance of sodium-ion battery was improved by using functionalized interconnected N-doped carbon nanofibers (FN-CNFs) as the anode.                         cas_cit50R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">50</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Use of Graphite as a Highly Reversible Electrode with Superior Cycle Life for Sodium-Ion Batteries by Making Use of Co-Intercalation Phenomena</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jache, Birte; Adelhelm, Philipp</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Although being the std. anode material in Li-ion batteries (LIBs), graphite so far is considered to fail application in Na-ion batteries (NIBs) because the Na-C system lacks suitable binary intercalation compds.  This limitation can be circumvented by using co-intercalation phenomena in a diglyme-based electrolyte.  The resulting compd. is a stage-I ternary intercalation compd. with an estd. stoichiometry of Na(diglyme)2C20.  Highlights of the electrode reaction are its high energy efficiency, the small irreversible loss during the 1st cycle, and a superior cycle life with capacities close to 100 mA-h g-1 for 1000 cycles and coulomb efficiencies &gt;99.87%.                         cas_cit52R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">52</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical insertion of sodium into hard carbons</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Thomas, P.; Billaud, D.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2002</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">47</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">20</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3303-3307</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ELCAAV</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4686</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Science Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. insertion of sodium ions into different types of hard carbons was achieved in electrolytes composed of ethylene carbonate as the solvent and NaClO4 as the salt.  For all the materials studied the sodium uptake increases when the carbon highest heat treatment temp. (HTT) decreases.  PAN-based carbon fibers appear to be suitable structures to allow significant sodium insertion.  Thus, T650 ex-PAN fibers lead to a reversible capacity close to 209 mAh g-1.  In that case, sodium insertion occurs in two main ways: one is the adsorption on the single graphene layers and the other is the concomitant insertion into the porosity that occurs below 0.1 V vs. Na+/Na.  This second mechanism, which is indicated by a low-voltage plateau on the electrochem. curves, allows significant insertion.  The compared electrochem. study of two saccharose-coke samples corresponding to different regions of Dahn's classification underlines the importance of the carbon precursor and of the manuf. process.  The reversible capacity is equal to 184 mAh g-1 for the sample heat treated at 800 °C which presents a high hydrogen content whereas it is close to 145 mAh g-1 for the one characterized by a HTT of about 1500 °C and a low hydrogen content.  The best electrochem. performances are obtained for pyrolyzed cellulose carbons.  Indeed, the reversible capacity is about 279 mAh g-1.  Outgassing these carbons at 950°C results in such a decrease of the reversible capacity down to 145 mAh g-1.  That can be related either to the thermal elimination of heteroelements or to modifications of the pore size distribution.  Consequently, the most suitable hard carbon material for anodic applications in rechargeable sodium-ion batteries should both present a high residual hydrogen content and a significant microporosity.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpJDGOzzH5pW7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgS0wQhOn1J1g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD38Xlslylsrg%253D&amp;md5=54dfbedb15fd432f5c3abc03b64cd1a2</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref53"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">53</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit53" data-doi="10.1021/nl3016957"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xiao, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sushko, M.                         cas_cit53R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">53</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium Ion Insertion in Hollow Carbon Nanowires for Battery Applications</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Cao, Yuliang; Xiao, Lifen; Sushko, Maria L.; Wang, Wei; Schwenzer, Birgit; Xiao, Jie; Nie, Zimin; Saraf, Laxmikant V.; Yang, Zhengguo; Liu, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hollow carbon nanowires were prepd. by pyrolysis of a hollow polyaniline nanowire precursor.  The hollow carbon nanowires used as anode material for Na-ion batteries deliver a high reversible capacity of 251 mA-h/g and 82.2% capacity retention over 400 charge-discharge cycles between 1.2 and 0.01 V (vs. Na+/Na) at a const. current of 50 mA/g (0.2C).                         cas_cit54R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">54</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Properties and sodium insertion behavior of Phenolic Resin-based hard carbon microspheres obtained by a hydrothermal method</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, He-liang; Shi, Zhi-qiang; Jin, Juan; Chong, Chuan-bin; Wang, Cheng-yang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Electroanalytical Chemistry</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Phenolic Resin-based hard C microspheres (PF-HCSs) were successfully synthesized by a simple hydrothermal route in a stainless steel autoclave and followed carbonization at different temps.  The materials possess perfect spherule shape and interlayer spacing above that of equil. graphite, facilitating Na intercalation and favoring efficient ion storage.  A high reversible capacity of 311 mAh g- 1 and good long-term electrochem. stability are achieved in the PF-HCS-1250 anode obtained at 1250°.  Carbonizing the samples at higher carbonization temp. than 1250° results in such a decrease of the reversible capacity down to 270 mAh g- 1.  That can be related to the appearing closed pore structure and the decreasing interlayer space which exceeds the limited value Na ion can insert.  Hence the most suitable hard C anode material for Na ion batteries should possess a large interlayer space and a suitable pore structure.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpBGzP-rgdAebVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgS0wQhOn1J1g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXht1Kru7vO&amp;md5=40edd45de6d1126b5e800058fe2b1e10</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref55"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">55</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit55" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.5b01349"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hasegawa, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Deguchi, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kanamori, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kobayashi, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kageyama, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Abe, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nakanishi, K.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High-Level Doping of Nitrogen, Phosphorus, and Sulfur into Activated Carbon Monoliths and Their Electrochemical Capacitances</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem.                         cas_cit55R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">55</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-Level Doping of Nitrogen, Phosphorus, and Sulfur into Activated Carbon Monoliths and Their Electrochemical Capacitances</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hasegawa, George; Deguchi, Takeru; Kanamori, Kazuyoshi; Kobayashi, Yoji; Kageyama, Hiroshi; Abe, Takeshi; Nakanishi, Kazuki</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The present report demonstrates a new technique for doping heteroatoms (nitrogen, phosphorus, and sulfur) into carbon materials via a versatile post-treatment.  The heat-treatment of carbon materials with a reagent, which is stable at ambient temps. and evolves reactive gases on heating, in a vacuum-closed tube allows the introduction of various heteroatom-contg. functional groups into a carbon matrix.  In addn., the sequential doping reactions give rise to dual- and triple-heteroatom-doped carbons.  The pore properties of the precursor carbon materials are preserved through each heteroatom doping process, which indicates that independent tuning of heteroatom doping and nanostructural morphol. can be achieved in various carbon materials.  The electrochem. investigation on the undoped and doped carbon monolithic electrodes applied to supercapacitors provides insights into the effects of heteroatom doping on electrochem. capacitance.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr9dtAGByPg_7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgS0wQhOn1J1g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhtVKisbvL&amp;md5=12f6bb893388cd14a308d504a138bbdd</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref56"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">56</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit56" data-doi="10.1002/celc.201500412"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hasegawa, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kanamori, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kannari, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ozaki, J.-I.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nakanishi, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Abe, T.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Hard Carbon Anodes for Na-Ion Batteries: Toward a Practical Use</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">ChemElectroChem</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">2</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">1917</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">1920</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbons have immense potential as anode materials for Na-ion batteries, because the expanded graphene interlayers and nanovoids between randomly stacked arom. fragments can accommodate a substantial amt. of sodium.  However, the large irreversible capacity in the first cycle still remains as a significant issue in terms of a practicable battery technol.  Here, we show that hard carbon electrodes derived from a common phenol resin deliver a high reversible capacity within the narrow potential range of 0.1-0.005 V (vs. Na+/Na) and an excellent initial coulombic efficiency up to 95 %.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium ion batteries (SIBs), based on earth-abundant and cost-effective elements, have attracted increasing attention.  Carbon-based materials are still the most potential anode materials for SIBs.  Because of insufficient interlayer spacing, graphite-based materials used currently in lithium ion batteries are not suitable for SIB anodes.  Herein, combining macro-construction and micro-modification, we design and prep. novel SN-co-doped hollow carbon spheres (SN-HCSs) by a new method.  The S and N play different roles during sodium ion storage.  Due to the hierarchical porous structure and heteroatoms including S and N, the SN-HCS anode exhibits superior performance, esp. with high rate capability (110 mA h/g at a c.d. of 10 A/g) and excellent cyclic stability (cycle as long as 2000 cycles with only 0.0195 mA h/g specific capacity decay for each cycle).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp6d9RCbWmggbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhtdg17KDXecg"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit58R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">58</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Hard Carbon Originated from Polyvinyl Chloride Nanofibers As High-Performance Anode Material for Na-Ion Battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Bai, Ying; Wang, Zhen; Wu, Chuan; Xu, Rui; Wu, Feng; Liu, Yuanchang; Li, Hui; Li, Yu; Lu, Jun; Amine, Khalil</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Two types of hard carbon materials were synthesized by direct pyrolysis of com. polyvinyl chloride (PVC) particles and pyrolysis of PVC nanofibers at 600-800°, resp., where the nanofibers were prepd. by an electrospinning PVC precursors method.  These as-prepd. hard carbon samples were used as anode materials for Na-ion batteries.  The hard carbon obtained from PVC nanofibers had a high reversible capacity of 271 mA-h/g and an initial coulombic efficiency of 69.9%, which were much superior to the one from com. PVC, namely, a reversible capacity of 206 mA-h/g and an initial coulombic efficiency of 60.9%.  In addn., the hard carbon obtained from the PVC nanofibers exhibited good cycling stability and rate performance: the initial discharge capacities were 389, 228, 194, 178, 147 mA-h/g at the c.d. of 12, 24, 60, 120, and 240 mA/g, resp., retaining 211 mA-h/g after 150 cycles.  Such excellent cycle performance, high reversible capacity, and good rate capability enabled this hard carbon to be a promising candidate as anode material for Na-ion battery application.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrPaHT0FB4U5bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhtdg17KDXecg"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit59R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">59</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Biomass derived hard carbon used as a high performance anode material for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hong, Kun-lei; Long, Qie; Zeng, Rui; Yi, Zi-qi; Zhang, Wei; Wang, Duo; Yin, Wei; Wu, Chao; Fan, Qing-jie; Zhang, Wu-xing; Huang, Yun-hui</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">32</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">12733-12738</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A porous hard carbon material was synthesized by the simple pyrolysis of H3PO4-treated biomass, i.e., pomelo peels, at 700 °C in N2.  The as-obtained hard carbon had a 3D connected porous structure and a large sp. surface area of 1272 m2 g-1.  XPS anal. showed that the carbon material was functionalized by O-contg. and P-contg. groups.  The porous hard carbon was used as an anode for sodium ion batteries and exhibited good cycling stability and rate capability, delivering a capacity of 181 mA h g-1 at 200 mA g-1 after 220 cycles and retaining a capacity of 71 mA h g-1 at 5 A g-1.  The sodium storage mechanisms of the porous hard carbon can be explained by Na+ intercalation into the disordered graphene layers, redox reaction of the surface O-contg. functional groups and Na+ storage in the nanoscale pores.  However, the porous hard carbon demonstrated a low coulombic efficiency of 27%, resulting from the formation of a solid electrolyte interphase film and the side reactions of surface phosphorus groups.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoldD5kAMcszLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhtdg17KDXecg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhtVKmtbrO&amp;md5=9b28ca12b1ea3a55e7e2ada7ae1e05f9</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref60"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref60'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">60</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit60" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2014.08.119"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jin, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yu, B.-J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shi, Z.-Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, C.-Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chong, C.-B.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Lignin-Based Electrospun Carbon Nanofibrous Webs as Free-Standing and Binder-Free Electrodes for Sodium Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.                 10.1016/j.jpowsour.2014.08.119</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D272%26publication_year%3D2014%26pages%3D800-807%26journal%3DJ.%2BPower%2BSources%26author%3DJ.%2BJin%26author%3DB.-J.%2BYu%26author%3DZ.-Q.%2BShi%26author%3DC.-Y.%2BWang%26author%3DC.-B.%2BChong%26title%3DLignin-Based%2BElectrospun%2BCarbon%2BNanofibrous%2BWebs%2Bas%2BFree-Standing%2Band%2BBinder-Free%2BElectrodes%2Bfor%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.jpowsour.2014.08.119&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsomega.7b00259&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.jpowsour.2014.08.119&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit60R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">60</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Lignin-based electrospun carbon nanofibrous webs as free-standing and binder-free electrodes for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jin, Juan; Yu, Bao-jun; Shi, Zhi-qiang; Wang, Cheng-yang; Chong, Chuan-bin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">272</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">800-807</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPSODZ</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Low-cost and bio-based carbon nanofibrous webs (PL-CNFs) are fabricated from polyacrylonitrile (PAN)-refined lignin (RL) which is extd. from hardwood lignosulfonate via simple eletrospinning followed by stabilization and carbonization.  The effects of the PAN/RL mass ratios varying from 9/1,7/3 to 5/5 and heat-treatment temps. (HTTs) in the range from 800, 1000 to 1300 °C on morphol. and structure of PL-CNFs are systematically studied.  Due to unique morphol. and weakly ordered turbostratic microstructure of the 3-D conductive composite networks, the PL-CNFs anode obtained at 1300 °C with a mass ratio of 5/5 exhibits a high reversible capacity of 292.6 mA h g-1 with an initial efficiency of 70.5% at a const. c.d. of 0.02 A g-1 when used as free-standing and binder-free anodes for sodium ion batteries (SIBs).  The anode also presents high rate capability (210 and 80 mA h g-1 at 0.4 and 1 A g-1, resp.) and excellent cycle stability (247 mA h g-1 reversible capacity with 90.2% capacity retention ratio at 0.1 A g-1 over 200 cycles).  It is demonstrated that biomass waste lignin can be applied as a promising precursor to fabricate low-cost-high performance carbon electrode materials for SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo6xoSPzjjd3bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhtdg17KDXecg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhsFCnsr3F&amp;md5=f017b8fdfb8a99b1a0c2b4e860724993</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref61"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref61'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">61</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit61" data-doi="10.1039/c5ta08601a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, Y.-S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Huang, X.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Superior Low-Cost Amorphous Carbon Anode Made from Pitch and Lignin for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J. Mater. Chem.                         cas_cit61R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">61</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A superior low-cost amorphous carbon anode made from pitch and lignin for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Yunming; Hu, Yong-Sheng; Li, Hong; Chen, Liquan; Huang, Xuejie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">96-104</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are a promising candidate for grid electricity storage due to their potential low cost.  The development of anode materials is a crucial step to promote the commercialization of SIBs, and amorphous carbon materials are likely to be the most promising alternatives among all proposed anode materials.  However, the cost of the reported carbon materials is still very high due to the expensive precursors and their low carbon yield.  Here, we report an amorphous carbon (AC) material made from low cost pitch.  The amorphous carbon material with an amazing high carbon yield of 57% was achieved by utilizing the emulsification interaction between pitch and lignin to suppress the graphitization of pitch during the carbonization.  The effects of heat-treatment temps. and the pitch/lignin mass ratios on the morphol., microstructure and the electrochem. performance of AC were systematically investigated.  By optimizing exptl. conditions, we achieved one representative AC with a suitable morphol. and microstructure, which exhibits promising performances with a high reversible capacity of 254 mA h g-1, a high initial coulombic efficiency of 82% and excellent cycling stability.  This is the first demonstration that the pitch can be successfully applied in fabricating amorphous carbon anode materials for SIBs with superior low cost and high performance.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqzLQKBB7Gqa7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhtdg17KDXecg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhslyksbnK&amp;md5=a8c8540d06fbad13618a164da2e6d5c5</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref62"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref62'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">62</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit62" data-doi="10.1039/C3TA12389H"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Luo, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Schardt, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bommier, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Razink, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Simonsen, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ji, X.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Carbon Nanofibers Derived from Cellulose Nanofibers as a Long-Life Anode Material for Rechargeable Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.                         cas_cit62R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">62</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Carbon nanofibers derived from cellulose nanofibers as a long-life anode material for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Luo, Wei; Schardt, Jenna; Bommier, Clement; Wang, Bao; Razink, Joshua; Simonsen, John; Ji, Xiulei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A highly reversible anode is indispensable to the future success of Na-ion batteries (SIBs).                         cas_cit63R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">63</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Interpretation of Raman spectra of disordered and amorphous carbon</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ferrari, A. C.; Robertson, J.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Review B: Condensed Matter and Materials Physics</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The model and theor. understanding of the Raman spectra in disordered and amorphous C are given.  The nature of the G and D vibration modes in graphite is analyzed in terms of the resonant excitation of π states and the long-range polarizability of π bonding.  Visible Raman data on disordered, amorphous, and diamondlike C are classified in a 3-stage model to show the factors that control the position, intensity, and widths of the G and D peaks.  The visible Raman spectra depend formally on the configuration of the sp2 sites in sp2-bonded clusters.  In cases where the sp2 clustering is controlled by the sp3 fraction, such as in as-deposited tetrahedral amorphous C (ta-C) or hydrogenated amorphous C (a-C:H) films, the visible Raman parameters can be used to derive the sp3 fraction.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqqdAvFvqG2KrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgjcQwzKMeiAg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD3cXjs1Smu7c%253D&amp;md5=e451e6f21e1f6cf375931e6a23e836bb</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref64"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref64'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">64</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit64" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2005.02.018"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sadezky, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Muckenhuber, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Grothe, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Niessner, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Pöschl, U.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Raman Microspectroscopy of Soot and Related Carbonaceous Materials: Spectral Analysis and Structural Information</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Carbon</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2005</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">43</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">1731</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">1742</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

Spectral analysis and structural information</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sadezky, A.; Muckenhuber, H.; Grothe, H.; Niessner, R.; Poeschl, U.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Exptl. conditions and math. fitting procedures for the collection and anal. of Raman spectra of soot and related carbonaceous materials were investigated and optimized with a Raman microscope system operated at 3 different laser excitation wavelengths (514, 633, and 780 nm).  Several band combinations for spectral anal. were tested, and a combination of 4 Lorentzian-shaped bands (G, D1, D2, D4) at about 1580, 1350, 1620, and 1200 cm-1, resp., with a Gaussian-shaped band (D3) at ∼1500 cm-1 was best suited for the 1st-order spectra.  The 2nd-order spectra were best fitted with Lorentzian-shaped bands at about 2450, 2700, 2900, and 3100 cm-1.  Spectral parameters (band positions, full widths at half max., and intensity ratios) are reported for several types of industrial C black (Degussa Printex, Cabot Monarch), diesel soot (particulate matter from modern heavy duty vehicle and passenger car engine exhaust, NIST SRM1650), spark-discharge soot (Palas GfG100), and graphite.  Several parameters, in particular the width of the D1 band at ∼1350 cm-1, provide structural information and allow to discriminate the sample materials, but the characterization and distinction of different types of soot is limited by the exptl. reproducibility of the spectra and the statistical uncertainties of curve fitting.  The results are discussed and compared with x-ray diffraction measurements and earlier Raman spectroscopic studies of comparable materials, where different measurement and fitting procedures was applied.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqI47SxmqUfxrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgjcQwzKMeiAg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD2MXltFGrtLc%253D&amp;md5=045e6d9ee2eb3d84bd001fca25bc7f62</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref65"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref65'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">65</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit65" data-doi="10.1021/jp1088788"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Du, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bie, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cai, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cui, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wei, Y.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Kinetics of the Li[Li<sub>0.23</sub>Co<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.47</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> Cathode Material Studied by GITT and EIS</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The Li[Li0.23Co0.3Mn0.47]O2 cathode material was prepd. by a sol-gel method.  Combination of x-ray diffraction and Raman scattering studies showed that the material was a solid soln. rather than a composite of nano Li2MnO3 and LiCoO2.  The material had a high discharge capacity of 250 mA-h/g in the voltage window of 2.0-4.8 V.  However, the capacity retention was poor.  The material showed different electrochem. mechanisms in the first charge and subsequent cycles. The Li+ diffusion coeffs. increased to 10-14 cm2/s after the first charge.  However, they were still much smaller than those of typical layered materials such as LiCoO2 and Li(Ni1/3Co1/3Mn1/3)O2.  Electrochem. impedance spectroscopy (EIS) study showed that the large interface impedance at high potential seriously hindered the electrode performance of the material.                         cas_cit66R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">66</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Analysis of the chemical diffusion coefficient of lithium ions in Li3V2(PO4)3 cathode material</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Rui, X.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The chem. diffusion coeffs. of Li ions ( DLi+ ) in Li3V2(PO4)3 between 3.0 and 4.8 V are systematically detd. by cyclic voltammetry (CV), electrochem. impedance spectroscopy (EIS) and galvanostatic intermittent titrn. technique (GITT). ( DLi+true) measured in single-phase region are at 10-9-10-10 cm2 s-1.  Its apparent diffusion coeffs. ( DLi+app) measured in 2-phase regions by CV and GITT range from 10-10 to 10-11 cm2 s-1 and 10-8 to 10-13 cm2 s-1, resp., depending on the potentials.  By the GITT, the DLi+ varies nonlinearly in a W shape with the charge-discharge voltage, which is ascribed to the strong interactions of Li+ with surrounding ions.         (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">15</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Nongraphitizable carbon, also known as hard carbon, is considered one of the most promising anodes for the emerging Na-ion batteries.  The current mechanistic understanding of Na-ion storage in hard carbon is based on the "card-house" model first raised in the early 2000s.  This model describes that Na-ion insertion occurs first through intercalation between graphene sheets in turbostratic nanodomains, followed by Na filling of the pores in the carbon structure.  We tried to test this model by tuning the sizes of turbostratic nanodomains but revealed a correlation between the structural defects and Na-ion storage.  Based on our exptl. data, we propose an alternative perspective for sodiation of hard carbon that consists of Na-ion storage at defect sites, by intercalation and last via pore-filling.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqegbEbsDPzrrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liPPew9dumt6g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXht1Oqsb3M&amp;md5=a2144d9fa8c9f19a479ebec0ec74f1eb</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref68"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref68'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">68</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit68" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201501874"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jian, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xing, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bommier, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ji, X.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Hard Carbon Microspheres: Potassium-Ion Anode Versus Sodium-Ion Anode</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.                 10.1002/aenm.201501874</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D6%26publication_year%3D2016%26pages%3D1501874%26journal%3DAdv.%2BEnergy%2BMater.%26author%3DZ.%2BJian%26author%3DZ.%2BXing%26author%3DC.%2BBommier%26author%3DZ.%2BLi%26author%3DX.%2BJi%26title%3DHard%2BCarbon%2BMicrospheres%253A%2BPotassium-Ion%2BAnode%2BVersus%2BSodium-Ion%2BAnode%26doi%3D10.1002%252Faenm.201501874&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsomega.7b00259&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Faenm.201501874&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref69"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref69'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">69</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit69" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201100854"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Komaba, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Murata, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ishikawa, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yabuuchi, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ozeki, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nakayama, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ogata, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gotoh, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fujiwara, K.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Na Insertion and Solid Electrolyte Interphase for Hard-Carbon Electrodes and Application to Na-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv. Funct. Mater.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2011</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">21</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">3859</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">3867</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/adfm.201100854</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D21%26publication_year%3D2011%26pages%3D3859-3867%26journal%3DAdv.%2BFunct.%2BMater.%26author%3DS.%2BKomaba%26author%3DW.%2BMurata%26author%3DT.%2BIshikawa%26author%3DN.%2BYabuuchi%26author%3DT.%2BOzeki%26author%3DT.%2BNakayama%26author%3DA.%2BOgata%26author%3DK.%2BGotoh%26author%3DK.%2BFujiwara%26title%3DElectrochemical%2BNa%2BInsertion%2Band%2BSolid%2BElectrolyte%2BInterphase%2Bfor%2BHard-Carbon%2BElectrodes%2Band%2BApplication%2Bto%2BNa-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fadfm.201100854&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsomega.7b00259&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fadfm.201100854&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit69R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">69</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical Na Insertion and Solid Electrolyte Interphase for Hard-Carbon Electrodes and Application to Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Komaba, Shinichi; Murata, Wataru; Ishikawa, Toru; Yabuuchi, Naoaki; Ozeki, Tomoaki; Nakayama, Tetsuri; Ogata, Atsushi; Gotoh, Kazuma; Fujiwara, Kazuya</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2011</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">21</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">20</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3859-3867</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AFMDC6</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1616-301X</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Recently, lithium-ion batteries were attracting more interest for use in automotive applications.  Lithium resources are confirmed to be unevenly distributed in South America, and the cost of the lithium raw materials has roughly doubled from the first practical application in 1991 to the present and is increasing due to global demand for lithium-ion accumulators.  Since the electrochem. equiv. and std. potential of sodium are the most advantageous after lithium, sodium based energy storage is of great interest to realize lithium-free high energy and high voltage batteries.  However, to the best of authors knowledge, there were no successful reports on electrochem. sodium insertion materials for battery applications; the major challenge is the neg. electrode and its passivation.  In this study, high capacity and excellent reversibility are achieved of sodium-insertion performance of hard-carbon and layered NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 electrodes in propylene carbonate electrolyte solns.  The structural change and passivation for hard-carbon are investigated to study the reversible sodium insertion.  The 3-V secondary Na-ion battery possessing environmental and cost friendliness, Na+-shuttlecock hard-carbon/NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 cell, demonstrates steady cycling performance as next generation secondary batteries and an alternative to Li-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrp5J70QNujrrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liPPew9dumt6g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXhtVWjurfM&amp;md5=be5cae72214fa773ada0e3fcca7b49d1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref70"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref70'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">70</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit70" data-doi="10.1149/1.1393348"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Stevens, D. A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dahn, J. R.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High Capacity Anode Materials for Rechargeable Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J. Electrochem. Soc.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2000</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">147</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">1271</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">1273</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1149/1.1393348</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D147%26publication_year%3D2000%26pages%3D1271-1273%26journal%3DJ.%2BElectrochem.%2BSoc.%26author%3DD.%2BA.%2BStevens%26author%3DJ.%2BR.%2BDahn%26title%3DHigh%2BCapacity%2BAnode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BRechargeable%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1149%252F1.1393348&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsomega.7b00259&amp;doiOfLink=10.1149%2F1.1393348&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit70R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">70</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High capacity anode materials for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Stevens, D. A.; Dahn, J. R.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2000</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">147</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1271-1273</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JESOAN</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4651</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Electrochem. techniques have been used to study the reversible insertion of sodium into hard-carbon host structures at room temp.  In this paper we compare these results with those for lithium insertion in the same materials and demonstrate the presence of similar alkali metal insertion mechanisms in both cases.  Despite the gravimetric capacities being lower for sodium than lithium insertion, we have achieved a reversible sodium capacity of 300 mAh/g, close to that for lithium insertion in graphitic carbon anode materials.  Such materials may therefore be useful as anodes in rechargeable sodium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpm125w9mEFSbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liPPew9dumt6g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD3cXisVygtL0%253D&amp;md5=7480ac0a7bc2620ce565b1c21f1e2463</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref71"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref71'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">71</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit71" data-doi="10.1149/1.1379565"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Stevens, D. A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dahn, J. R.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The Mechanisms of Lithium and Sodium Insertion in Carbon Materials</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J. Electrochem. Soc.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2001</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">148</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">A803</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">A811</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1149/1.1379565</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D148%26publication_year%3D2001%26pages%3DA803-A811%26journal%3DJ.%2BElectrochem.%2BSoc.%26author%3DD.%2BA.%2BStevens%26author%3DJ.%2BR.%2BDahn%26title%3DThe%2BMechanisms%2Bof%2BLithium%2Band%2BSodium%2BInsertion%2Bin%2BCarbon%2BMaterials%26doi%3D10.1149%252F1.1379565&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsomega.7b00259&amp;doiOfLink=10.1149%2F1.1379565&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit71R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">71</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The mechanisms of lithium and sodium insertion in carbon materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Stevens, D. A.; Dahn, J. R.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2001</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">148</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">A803-A811</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JESOAN</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4651</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors compare the interactions of lithium and sodium with a range of carbon materials in electrochem. cells.  Through wide angle in situ x-ray scattering studies, both lithium and sodium can be inserted into the interlayer space in disordered carbon materials.  This insertion process is accompanied by an increase in the interlayer spacing in these materials.  Small-angle in situ scattering studies are presented to clearly show the insertion of lithium and sodium into nanopores within disordered hard carbons.  Also very little, if any, sodium can be inserted into graphitic materials in contrast to the large capacity seen for lithium insertion.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqOuo4QAURxurVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liPPew9dumt6g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Ever-growing energy needs and depleting fossil-fuel resources demand the pursuit of sustainable energy alternatives, including both renewable energy sources and sustainable storage technologies.  It is therefore essential to incorporate material abundance, eco-efficient synthetic processes and life-cycle anal. into the design of new electrochem. storage systems.  At present, a few existing technologies address these issues, but in each case, fundamental and technol. hurdles remain to be overcome.  Here the authors provide an overview of the current state of energy storage from a sustainability perspective.  The authors introduce the notion of sustainability through discussion of the energy and environmental costs of state-of-the-art Li-ion batteries, considering elemental abundance, toxicity, synthetic methods and scalability.  With the same themes in mind, the authors also highlight current and future electrochem. storage systems beyond Li-ion batteries.  The complexity and importance of recycling battery materials is also discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGph4-d2B3Q8DLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhf3PyJIr2AuQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhvFKlsrjF&amp;md5=977f0fc171bb41af30457715fc0ded72</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1039/C2EE02781J"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Palomares, V.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Serras, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Villaluenga, I.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hueso, K.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoRYtxfVVW_sLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgGm-wtps8wGg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XivFWnu7s%253D&amp;md5=72869139086b8ee9adec746418ff84b1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1021/cr500192f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yabuuchi, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kubota, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dahbi, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Komaba, S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Research Development on Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem.                         rightTab-cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent Advances and Prospects of Cathode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xiang, Xingde; Zhang, Kai; Chen, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Na-ion batteries (SIBs) receive significant attention for electrochem. energy storage and conversion owing to their wide availability and the low cost of Na resources.  However, SIBs face challenges of low specific energy, short cycling life, and insufficient specific power, owing to the heavy mass and large radius of Na+ ions.  As an important component of SIBs, cathode materials have a significant effect on the SIB electrochem. performance.  The most recent advances and prospects of inorg. and org. cathode materials are summarized here.  Among current cathode materials, layered transition-metal oxides achieve high specific energies around 600 mW h g-1 owing to their high specific capacities of 180-220 mA h g-1 and their moderate operating potentials of 2.7-3.2 V (vs. Na+/Na).  Porous Na3V2(PO4)3/C nanomaterials exhibit excellent cycling performance with almost 100% retention over 1000 cycles owing to their robust structural framework.  Recent emerging cathode materials, such as amorphous NaFePO4 and pteridine derivs. show interesting electrochem. properties and attractive prospects for application in SIBs.  Future work should focus on strategies to enhance the overall performance of cathode materials in terms of specific energy, cycling life, and rate capability with cationic doping, anionic substitution, morphol. fabrication, and electrolyte matching.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoRG1Bub04s8LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgGm-wtps8wGg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhtlCjur7N&amp;md5=6d0a96f9e9421c02a9f3b48744267752</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref6" class="rightTabRefNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit6" data-doi="10.1016/j.elecom.2013.11.015"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Vassilaras, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Toumar, A.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Most P2-type layered oxides exhibit Na+/vacancy-ordered superstructures because of strong Na+-Na+ interaction in the alkali metal layer and charge ordering in the transition metal layer.  These superstructures evidenced by voltage plateaus in the electrochem. curves limit the Na+ ion transport kinetics and cycle performance in rechargeable batteries.  Here we show that such Na+/vacancy ordering can be avoided by choosing the transition metal ions with similar ionic radii and different redox potentials, for example, Cr3+ and Ti4+.  The designed P2-Na0.6[Cr0.6Ti0.4]O2 is completely Na+/vacancy-disordered at any sodium content and displays excellent rate capability and long cycle life.  A sym. sodium-ion battery using the same P2-Na0.6[Cr0.6Ti0.4]O2 electrode delivers 75% of the initial capacity at 12C rate.  Our contribution demonstrates that the approach of preventing Na+/vacancy ordering by breaking charge ordering in the transition metal layer opens a simple way to design disordered electrode materials with high power d. and long cycle life.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrlj5SkXA-GV7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgGm-wtps8wGg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhtF2ksrfJ&amp;md5=719e1ce8412c6a1d17ca3f1b29c3314e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref8" class="rightTabRefNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit8" data-doi="10.1021/cm403855t"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lee, D.         (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">26</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Li-substituted layered P2-Na0.80[Li0.12Ni0.22Mn0.66]O2 is studied as an advanced cathode material for Na-ion batteries.  Both n diffraction and NMR spectroscopy are used to elucidate the local structure, and they reveal that most of the Li ions are located in transition metal (TM) sites, preferably surrounded by Mn ions.  To characterize structural changes occurring upon electrochem. cycling, in situ synchrotron x-ray diffraction is conducted.  No significant phase transformation is obsd. up to 4.4 V charge for this material, unlike Li-free P2-type Na cathodes.  The presence of monovalent Li ions in the TM layers allows more Na ions to reside in the prismatic sites, stabilizing the overall charge balance of the compd.  Consequently, more Na ions remain in the compd. upon charge, the P2 structure is retained in the high voltage region, and the phase transformation is delayed.  Ex situ NMR is conducted on samples at different states of charge/discharge to track Li-ion site occupation changes.  Surprisingly, Li is mobile, some Li ions migrate from the TM layer to the Na layer at high voltage, and yet this process is highly reversible.  Novel design principles for Na cathode materials are proposed from an atomistic level understanding of the underlying electrochem. processes.  These principles enable one to devise an optimized, high capacity, and structurally stable compd. as a potential cathode material for high-energy Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoRaU2CEikYCrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liQ82xpdvXPcw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhvVSluw%253D%253D&amp;md5=aebb4423baa75fe925ec0b0c2f7c0955</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref9" class="rightTabRefNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit9" data-doi="10.1038/nmat3309"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yabuuchi, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kajiyama, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Iwatate, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nishikawa, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hitomi, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Okuyama, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Usui, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yamada, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Komaba, S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">P2-type Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>[Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> Made from Earth-Abundant Elements for Rechargeable Na Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nat.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The aq. sodium-ion battery system is a safe and low-cost soln. for large-scale energy storage, because of the abundance of sodium and inexpensive aq. electrolytes.  Although several pos. electrode materials, for example, Na0.44MnO2, were proposed, few neg. electrode materials, for example, activated carbon and NaTi2(PO4)3, are available.  Here we show that Ti-substituted Na0.44MnO2 (Na0.44[Mn1-xTix]O2) with tunnel structure can be used as a neg. electrode material for aq. sodium-ion batteries. Ti substitution tunes the charge ordering property and reaction pathway, significantly smoothing the discharge/charge profiles and lowering the storage voltage.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-rich prussian blue analogs (PBs) with high coulombic efficiency are of fundamental and technol. importance for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  Here we report high-quality NaxFeFe(CN)6 nanocubes as cathode materials for SIBs and investigate their sodium storage mechanism.  Among them, Na-rich Na1.70FeFe(CN)6 shows highly reversible electrochem. reactions, delivering a capacity as high as 120.7 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 200 mA g-1; even at 1200 mA g-1, the capacity still retains up to 73.6 mA h g-1. Exptl. results and first-principle calcns. demonstrate that sodium cations in the large cavities of PBs have a priority to occupy the 8c site, while in the Na-rich samples, Na+ ions can be pushed into other 24d site.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium is globally available, which makes a sodium-ion rechargeable battery preferable to a lithium-ion battery for large-scale storage of elec. energy, provided a host cathode for Na can be found that provides the necessary capacity, voltage, and cycle life at the prescribed charge/discharge rate.  Low-cost hexacyanometallates are promising cathodes because of their ease of synthesis and rigid open framework that enables fast Na+ insertion and extn.  Here an intriguing effect is reported of interstitial H2O on the structure and electrochem. properties of sodium manganese(II) hexacyanoferrates(II) with the nominal compn. Na2MnFe(CN)6·zH2O (Na2-δMnHFC).  The newly discovered dehydrated Na2-δMnHFC phase exhibits superior electrochem. performance compared to other reported Na-ion cathode materials; it delivers at 3.5 V a reversible capacity of 150 mAh g-1 in a sodium half cell and 140 mAh g-1 in a full cell with a hard-carbon anode.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium iron hexacyanoferrate (Fe-HCF) is a potential cathode material for sodium-ion batteries.  However, the side reactions in the NaClO4 electrolyte in the charge process over 4.1 V result in structural collapse and low coulombic efficiency during cycling.  In this work, we prepd. high-quality Fe-HCF microcubes with a size distribution of ∼2 μm and employed conductive polypyrrole (PPy) to wrap the microcubes to form a Fe-HCF@PPy composite.  With the coating of PPy, the composite exhibits a discharge capacity of 113.0 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 25 mA g-1 and 75 mA h g-1 at 3000 mA g-1.  A greatly improved cycling stability is attained with 79% capacity retention over 500 cycles at 200 mA g-1.  The superior rate capability and excellent cyclability can be ascribed to the effects from PPy as both an electronic conductor to enhance the cond. and a protective layer to prevent the side reactions.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpnhwRtM8-RcbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj1xNQFUxRjLg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XksFOitrs%253D&amp;md5=6ef39b1213e5bdb6cfe4a2700f1c63a4</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref21" class="rightTabRefNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit21" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.5b12620"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wei, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Qian, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ai, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, H.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Highly Crystallized Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> with Suppressed Lattice Defects as Superior Cathode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Prussian blue and its analogs have received particular attention as superior cathodes for Na-ion batteries due to their potential 2-Na storage capacity (∼170 mA-h g-1) and low cost.         </div><div class="casAbstract">NASICON-structured Na3V2(PO4)2F3 is considered as a potentially high-capacity cathode material for Na-ion batteries; however, its poor rate capability and insufficient cyclability remain a challenge for battery applications.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered a top alternative to Li-ion batteries (LIBs) for large-scale renewable energy storage units due to their low cost and the abundance of Na-bearing precursors in the earth's mineral deposits.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Antimony/carbon (Sb@C) microspheres are initially synthesized via a facile self-catalyzing solvothermal method, and their applicability as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries is investigated.
